A [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Federal_parliamentary_republic"] federal parliamentary republic state, Pakistan is a federation that comprises [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Four_Provinces_(Pakistan)"] four provinces: Punjab, Khyber
Pakhtunkhwa, Sindh and Balochistan,[226] and three territories: [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Islamabad_Capital_Territory"] Islamabad Capital Territory, Gilgit-Baltistan and Azad
Kashmir. The [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Government_of_Pakistan"] Government of Pakistan exercises the [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/De_facto_standard"] de facto jurisdiction over the [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Frontier_Regions"] Frontier Regions and the [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Western_Kashmir"] western parts
of the [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kashmir_Region"] Kashmir Regions, which are organised into the separate political entities Azad Kashmir and Gilgit-Baltistan
(formerly Northern Areas). In 2009, the [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Constitution_of_Pakistan"] constitutional assignment (the Gilgit–Baltistan Empowerment and Self-Governance
Order) awarded the Gilgit-Baltistan a [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Provinces_of_Pakistan"] semi-provincial status, giving it self-government.[227]
The [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Local_government_in_Pakistan"] local government system consists of a three-tier system of districts, tehsils, and [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Union_Councils_of_Pakistan"] union councils, with an elected body at
each tier.[228] There are about 130 districts altogether, of which Azad Kashmir has ten[229] and Gilgit-Baltistan seven.[230]
Clickable map of the four provinces and three federal territories of Pakistan.
Page 14
 Law enforcement is carried out by a joint network of the [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pakistan_Intelligence_Community"] intelligence community with jurisdiction limited to the relevant
province or territory. The [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/National_Intelligence_Directorate_(Pakistan)"] National Intelligence Directorate coordinates the information intelligence at both federal and
provincial levels; including the FIA, IB, [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/National_Highways_and_Motorway_Police"] Motorway Police, and [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Paramilitary_forces_of_Pakistan"] paramilitary forces such as the [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pakistan_Rangers"] Pakistan Rangers and the
 Frontier Corps.[231]
Pakistan's "premier" intelligence agency, the [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Inter-Services_Intelligence"] Inter-Services Intelligence (ISI), was formed just within a year after the
Independence of Pakistan in 1947.[232] ABC News Point in 2014 reported that the ISI was ranked as the top intelligence
agency in the world[233] while [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Zee_News"] Zee News reported the ISI as ranking fifth among the world's most powerful intelligence
agencies.[234]
 The court system is organised as a hierarchy, with the Supreme Court at the apex, below which are high courts, Federal
Shariat Courts (one in each province and one in the federal capital), [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/District_courts_of_Pakistan"] district courts (one in each district), Judicial Magistrate
Courts (in every town and city), Executive Magistrate Courts, and civil courts. The [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pakistan_penal_code"] Penal code has limited jurisdiction in
the Tribal Areas, where law is largely derived from tribal customs.[231][235]
Kashmir conflict
 Kashmir, a Himalayan region situated at the northernmost point of the Indian
 subcontinent, was governed as an autonomous princely state known as Jammu and
Kashmir in the [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/British_Raj"] British Raj prior to the [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Partition_of_India"] Partition of India in August 1947. Following
the independence of India and Pakistan post-partition, the region became the subject
of a [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kashmir_conflict"] major territorial dispute that has hindered [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/India%E2%80%93Pakistan_relations"] their bilateral relations. The two states
have engaged each other in [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Indo-Pakistani_wars_and_conflicts"] two large-scale wars over the region in [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Indo-Pakistani_War_of_1947%E2%80%931948"] 1947–1948 and
1965. India and Pakistan have also fought smaller-scale protracted conflicts over the
region in [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Siachen_conflict"] 1984 and 1999.[179] Approximately 45.1% of the Kashmir region is
controlled by India (administratively split into [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jammu_and_Kashmir_(union_territory)"] Jammu and Kashmir and Ladakh),
which also claims the entire territory of the former princely state of Jammu and
Kashmir that is not under its control.[179] India's control over Jammu and Kashmir
and Ladakh as well as its claim to the rest of the region has likewise been contested
by Pakistan, which controls approximately 38.2% of the region (administratively split
into [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Azad_Kashmir"] Azad Jammu and Kashmir and Gilgit−Baltistan) and claims all of the territory
under Indian control.[179][236] Additionally, approximately 20% of the region has 
The areas shown in green are the
Pakistani-controlled areas.
been controlled by [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/China"] China (known as [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Aksai_Chin"] Aksai Chin and the [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Trans-Karakoram_Tract"] Shaksgam Valley) since the
 Sino-Indian War of 1962 and the [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sino-Pakistan_Agreement"] Sino-Pakistani Agreement of 1963.[237] The Chinese-controlled areas of Kashmir
remain subject to an Indian territorial claim, but are not claimed by Pakistan.
India claims the entire Kashmir region on the basis of the [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Instrument_of_Accession_(Jammu_and_Kashmir)"] Instrument of Accession—a legal agreement with the princely
state of Jammu and Kashmir that was executed by [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Maharaja_Hari_Singh"] Hari Singh, the [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Maharaja"] maharaja of the state, who agreed to cede the entire area
to newly-independent India.[238] Pakistan claims most of Kashmir on the basis of its Muslim-majority population and of its
geography, the same principles that were applied for the creation of the two independent states.[239] India referred the
dispute to the [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/United_Nations"] United Nations on 1 January 1948.[240] In a [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/United_Nations_Security_Council_Resolution_38"] resolution passed in 1948, the UN's [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/United_Nations_General_Assembly"] General Assembly asked
Page 15
Pakistan to remove most of its military troops to set the conditions for the holding
of a plebiscite. However, Pakistan failed to vacate the region and a [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karachi_Agreement"] ceasefire was
reached in 1949 establishing a ceasefire line known as the [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Line_of_Control"] Line of Control (LoC)
that divided Kashmir between the two states as a [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/De_facto"] de facto border.[241] India,
fearful that the Muslim-majority populace of Kashmir would vote to secede from
India, did not allow a plebiscite to take place in the region. This was confirmed in
a statement by India's Defense Minister, [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/V._K._Krishna_Menon"] Krishna Menon, who stated: "Kashmir
would vote to join Pakistan and no Indian Government responsible for agreeing to
 Hunza Valley in the Gilgit-Baltistan plebiscite would survive."[242]
region is part of Pakistani-controlled
Kashmir.  Pakistan claims that its position is for the right of the [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kashmiris"] Kashmiri people to determine
their future through impartial elections as mandated by the United Nations,[243]
while India has stated that Kashmir is an "[URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Akhand_Bharat"] integral part" of India, referring to the
 1972 Simla Agreement and to the fact that [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Elections_in_Jammu_and_Kashmir"] regional elections take place regularly.[244] In recent developments, certain
Kashmiri independence groups believe that Kashmir should be independent of both India and Pakistan.[179]
Law enforcement
 The law enforcement in Pakistan is carried out by joint network of several federal and provincial police agencies. The four
provinces and the [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Islamabad_Capital_Territory"] Islamabad Capital Territory (ICT) each have a civilian police force with jurisdiction extending only to
the relevant province or territory.[150] At the federal level, there are a number of civilian [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pakistan_Intelligence_Community"] intelligence agencies with
nationwide jurisdictions including the [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Federal_Investigation_Agency"] Federal Investigation Agency (FIA) and the [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Intelligence_Bureau_(Pakistan)"] Intelligence Bureau (IB), as well as
several [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Paramilitary_forces_of_Pakistan"] paramilitary forces such as the [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/National_Guard_of_Pakistan"] National Guards ([URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Northern_Areas_of_Pakistan"] Northern Areas), the [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pakistan_Rangers"] Rangers (Punjab and Sindh), and the
 Frontier Corps (Khyber Pakhtunkhwa and Balochistan).
The most senior officers of all the civilian police forces also form part of the [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Police_Service_of_Pakistan"] Police Service, which is a component of the
 civil service of Pakistan. Namely, there is four provincial [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Police_Service_of_Pakistan"] police service including the [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Punjab_Police_(Pakistan)"] Punjab Police, [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sindh_Police"] Sindh Police,
 Khyber-Pakhtunkhwa Police, and the [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Balochistan_Police"] Balochistan Police; all headed by the appointed senior [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Inspector_general"] Inspector-Generals. The ICT
 has its own police component, the Capital Police, to maintain law and order in the capital. The CID bureaus are the crime
investigation unit and form a vital part in each provincial [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Police_Service_of_Pakistan"] police service.
The [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Law_enforcement_in_Pakistan"] law enforcement in Pakistan also has a [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/National_Highways_%26_Motorway_Police"] Motorway Patrol which is responsible for enforcement of traffic and safety
laws, security and recovery on Pakistan's inter-provincial [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Motorways_of_Pakistan"] motorway network. In each of provincial [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Police_Service_of_Pakistan"] Police Service, it also
maintains a respective [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Elite_Police"] Elite Police units led by the NACTA—a counter-terrorism police unit as well as providing VIP
escorts. In the Punjab and Sindh, the [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pakistan_Rangers"] Pakistan Rangers are an internal security force with the prime objective to provide
and maintain security in war zones and areas of conflict as well as maintaining law and order which includes providing
assistance to the police.[245] The [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Frontier_Corps"] Frontier Corps serves the similar purpose in [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Khyber_Pakhtunkhwa"] Khyber-Pakhtunkhwa, and the
Balochistan.[245]
Human rights
Male [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Homosexuality"] homosexuality is illegal in Pakistan and punishable with up to life in prison.[246] In its 2018 [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Press_Freedom_Index"] Press Freedom Index,
 Reporters Without Borders ranked Pakistan number 139 out of 180 countries based on [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Freedom_of_the_press"] freedom of the press.[247]
Television stations and newspapers are routinely shut down for publishing any reports critical of the government or the
military.[248]
Military
The armed forces of Pakistan are the [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_countries_by_number_of_troops"] sixth largest in the world in terms of numbers in full-time service, with about 651,800
 personnel on active duty and 291,000 paramilitary personnel, as of tentative estimates in 2021.[249] They came into
existence after independence in 1947, and the military establishment has frequently influenced the [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Politics_of_Pakistan"] national politics ever
since.[174] [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chain_of_command"] Chain of command of the military is kept under the control of the [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Joint_Chiefs_of_Staff_Committee"] Joint Chiefs of Staff Committee; all of the
 branches joint works, co-ordination, military logistics, and joint missions are under the Joint Staff HQ.[250] The Joint Staff
HQ is composed of the [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/AHQ_(PAF)"] Air HQ, [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/NHQ_(PN)"] Navy HQ, and [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/General_Headquarters_(Pakistan_Army)"] Army GHQ in the vicinity of the [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rawalpindi_Cantonment"] Rawalpindi Military District.[251]
Page 16
The [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chairman_Joint_Chiefs_of_Staff_Committee"] Chairman Joint Chiefs of Staff Committee is the highest [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Staff_Officer"] principle staff officer
in the armed forces, and the chief military adviser to the [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Government_of_Pakistan"] civilian government
though the chairman has no authority over the three branches of armed forces.[250]
The [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chairman_Joint_Chiefs_of_Staff_Committee"] Chairman joint chiefs controls the military from the [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Joint_Staff_Headquarters_(Pakistan)"] JS HQ and maintains
strategic communications between the military and the civilian government.[250]
 As of 2021, the CJCSC is General Nadeem Raza[252] alongside chief of army
 staff General Qamar Javed Bajwa,[253] chief of naval staff Admiral Muhammad
 Amjad Khan Niazi,[254] and chief of air staff Air Chief Marshal Zaheer Ahmad
Babar.[255] The main branches are the Army, the [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pakistan_Air_Force"] Air Force and the Navy, which
are supported by a large number of [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Paramilitary_forces_of_Pakistan"] paramilitary forces in the country.[256] Control
 over the strategic arsenals, deployment, employment, development, military
 computers and command and control is a responsibility vested under the National
Command Authority which oversaw the work on the [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nuclear_Doctrine_of_Pakistan"] nuclear policy as part of the
credible [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/N-deterrence"] minimum deterrence.[108] 
 Pakistan Air Force's [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/JF-17_Thunder"] JF-17 Thunder
flying in front of the 8,130-metre-high
(26,660-foot) [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nanga_Parbat"] Nanga Parbat
The United States, Turkey, and China maintain close military relations and regularly export military equipment and
 technology transfer to Pakistan.[257] Joint logistics and major [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Military_exercise"] war games are occasionally carried out by the militaries of
China and Turkey.[256][258] Philosophical basis for the [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Conscription"] military draft is introduced by the [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Constitution_of_Pakistan"] Constitution in times of
emergency, but it has never been imposed.[259]
Military history
Since 1947 Pakistan has been involved in [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Indo-Pakistani_wars_and_conflicts"] four [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Conventional_warfare"] conventional wars. [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Indo-Pakistani_War_of_1947%E2%80%931948"] The first occurred in Kashmir with Pakistan gaining
control of [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Western_Kashmir"] Western Kashmir, (Azad Kashmir and Gilgit-Baltistan), and India retaining [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Eastern_Kashmir"] Eastern Kashmir (Jammu and
 Kashmir and Ladakh). Territorial problems eventually led to another conventional war in 1965. The issue of Bengali
refugees led to another war in 1971 which resulted in Pakistan's [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pakistani_Instrument_of_Surrender"] unconditional surrender in East Pakistan.[260] Tensions in
Kargil brought the two countries at the [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Indo-Pakistani_War_of_1999"] brink of war.[109] Since 1947 the unresolved [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Durand_Line"] territorial problems with [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Afghanistan"] Afghanistan
[URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pakistani_intelligence_community"] saw border skirmishes which were kept mostly at the mountainous border. In 1961, the military and intelligence
community repelled the [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Military_history_of_Pakistan#Pakistan-Afghanistan_border_clash_of_1961"] Afghan incursion in the [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bajaur_Agency"] Bajaur Agency near the [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Durand_Line"] Durand Line border.[261]
Rising tensions with neighbouring USSR in [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet%E2%80%93Afghan_War"] their involvement in Afghanistan, [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pakistani_intelligence_community"] Pakistani intelligence community, mostly
the ISI, [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Operation_Cyclone"] systematically coordinated the [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/United_States_involvement_in_regime_change"] US resources to the [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Afghan_mujahideen"] Afghan mujahideen and [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Afghan_Arabs"] foreign fighters against the Soviet
Union's presence in the region. Military reports indicated that the [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pakistan_Air_Force"] PAF was in engagement with the [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Air_Forces"] Soviet Air Force,
supported by the [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Afghan_Air_Force"] Afghan Air Force during the course of the conflict; one of which belonged to [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_Rutskoy"] Alexander Rutskoy.[262]
Apart from its own conflicts, Pakistan has been an active [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/United_Nations_peacekeeping_missions_involving_Pakistan"] participant in United Nations peacekeeping missions. It played a
 major role in rescuing trapped American soldiers from Mogadishu, Somalia, in 1993 in Operation Gothic
 Serpent.[263][264] According to UN reports, the Pakistani military is the third largest troop contributor to UN peacekeeping
missions after [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ethiopia"] Ethiopia and India.[265]
Pakistan has [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pakistan_Armed_Forces_deployments"] deployed its military in some [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arab_world"] Arab countries, providing defence, training, and playing advisory roles.[266]
The [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pakistan_Air_Force"] PAF and [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pakistan_Navy"] Navy's fighter pilots have voluntarily served in Arab nations' militaries against Israel in [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Six-Day_War"] the Six-Day War
(1967) and in the [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Yom_Kippur_War"] Yom Kippur War (1973). Pakistan's [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fighter_pilot"] fighter pilots shot down ten Israeli planes in the Six-Day War.[263]
In the 1973 war, one of the PAF pilots, Flt. Lt. [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sattar_Alvi"] Sattar Alvi (flying a MiG-21), shot down an Israeli Air Force Mirage and
was honoured by the Syrian government.[267] Requested by the [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Government_of_Saudi_Arabia"] Saudi monarchy in 1979, Pakistan's [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Special_Service_Group"] special forces units,
operatives, and commandos were rushed to assist [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Saudi_forces"] Saudi forces in [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mecca"] Mecca to [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Grand_Mosque_Seizure"] lead the operation of the [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Masjid_al-Haram"] Grand Mosque. For
 almost two weeks Saudi Special Forces and Pakistani commandos fought the insurgents who had occupied the Grand
Mosque's compound.[268] In 1991, Pakistan became involved with the [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gulf_War"] Gulf War and sent 5,000 troops as part of a US-led
coalition, specifically for the defence of [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Saudi_Arabia"] Saudi Arabia.[269]
Despite the UN arms embargo on Bosnia, General [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Javed_Nasir"] Javed Nasir of the [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Inter-Services_Intelligence"] ISI airlifted anti-tank weapons and missiles to
Bosnian mujahideen which turned the tide in favour of Bosnian Muslims and forced the Serbs to lift the siege. Under
Nasir's leadership the [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Inter-Services_Intelligence"] ISI was also involved in supporting Chinese Muslims in [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Xinjiang"] Xinjiang Province, rebel Muslim groups in
the Philippines, and some religious groups in Central Asia.[270]
Since 2004, the military has been engaged in an [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/War_in_North-West_Pakistan"] insurgency in the Khyber Pakhtunkhwa province, mainly against the
 Tehrik-i-Taliban factions.[271] Major operations undertaken by the army include [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Operation_Black_Thunderstorm"] Operation Black Thunderstorm,
 Operation Rah-e-Nijat and [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Operation_Zarb-e-Azb"] Operation Zarb-e-Azb.[272]
Page 17
According to SIPRI, Pakistan was the 9th-largest recipient and importer of arms between 2012 and 2016.[273]
Economy

power parity (PPP), and [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_countries_by_GDP_(nominal)"] 42nd-largest in terms of nominal [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gross_domestic_product"] gross domestic product.

throughout the first millennium CE, with the largest economy by GDP. This

The structure of the Pakistani economy has changed from [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Agriculture_in_Pakistan"] a mainly agricultural to a
strong service base. Agriculture as of 2015 accounts for only 20.9% of the GDP.[313]
Even so, according to the United Nations [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Food_and_Agriculture_Organization"] Food and Agriculture Organization,
Pakistan produced 21,591,400 metric tons of wheat in 2005, more than all of Africa
(20,304,585 metric tons) and nearly as much as all of South America (24,557,784
metric tons).[314] Majority of the population, directly or indirectly, is dependent on
this sector. It accounts for 43.5% of employed labour force and is the largest source of
foreign exchange earnings.[313][315]  [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Surface_mining"] Surface mining in Sindh.
A large portion of the country's manufactured exports is dependent on raw materials
such as cotton and hides that are part of the agriculture sector, while supply shortages
and market disruptions in farm products do push up inflationary pressures. The 
Pakistan has been termed the
'Saudi Arabia of Coal' by
 Forbes.[312]
country is also the fifth-largest producer of cotton, with cotton production of
14 million bales from a modest beginning of 1.7 million bales in the early 1950s; is self-sufficient in sugarcane; and is the
fourth-largest producer in the world of milk. Land and water resources have not risen proportionately, but the increases
have taken place mainly due to gains in labour and agriculture productivity. The major breakthrough in crop production
took place in the late 1960s and 1970s due to the [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Green_Revolution"] Green Revolution that made a significant contribution to land and yield
increases of wheat and rice. Private tube wells led to a 50 percent increase in the cropping intensity which was augmented
by tractor cultivation. While the tube wells raised crop yields by 50 percent, the High Yielding Varieties (HYVs) of wheat
and rice led to a 50–60 percent higher yield.[316] Meat industry accounts for 1.4 percent of overall GDP.[317]
Industry
Industry is the second-largest sector of the economy, accounting for 19.74% of gross
domestic product (GDP),  and 24 percent of total employment. Large-scale
manufacturing (LSM), at 12.2% of GDP, dominates the overall sector, accounting for
66% of the sectoral share, followed by small-scale manufacturing, which accounts for
4.9% of total GDP. Pakistan's cement industry is also fast growing mainly because of
demand from [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Afghanistan"] Afghanistan and from the domestic real estate sector. In 2013 Pakistan
exported 7,708,557 [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Metric_tons"] metric tons of cement.[319] Pakistan has an installed capacity of
44,768,250 metric tons of cement and 42,636,428 metric tons of clinker. In 2012 and
2013, the cement industry in Pakistan became the most profitable sector of the
economy.[320]
The [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Textile_industry_in_Pakistan"] textile industry has a pivotal position in the manufacturing sector of Pakistan. In
Asia, Pakistan is the eighth-largest exporter of textile products, contributing 9.5% to
the GDP and providing employment to around 15 million people (some 30% of the
49 million people in the workforce). Pakistan is the fourth-largest producer of cotton
with the third-largest spinning capacity in Asia after China and India, contributing 5%
to the global spinning capacity.[321] China is the second largest buyer of Pakistani 
Television assembly factory in
Lahore. Pakistan's industrial
sector accounts for about 20.3%
of the GDP, and is dominated by
 small and medium-sized
enterprises.[318]
textiles, importing US$1.527 billion of textiles last fiscal. Unlike the US, where
mostly value-added textiles are imported, China buys only cotton yarn and cotton fabric from Pakistan. In 2012, Pakistani
textile products accounted for 3.3% or US$1.07bn of all UK textile imports, 12.4% or $4.61bn of total Chinese textile
imports, 3.0% of all US textile imports ($2,980 million), 1.6% of total German textile imports ($880 million) and 0.7% of
total Indian textile imports ($888 million).[322]

Services
The services sector makes up 58.8% of GDP[313] and has emerged as
the main driver of economic growth.[323] Pakistani society like other
developing countries is a consumption oriented society, having a high
marginal propensity to consume. The growth rate of services sector is
higher than the growth rate of agriculture and industrial sector. Services
sector accounts for 54 percent of GDP in 2014 and little over one-third Rising skyline of [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karachi"] Karachi with several under
of total employment. Services sector has strong linkages with other construction skyscrapers.
sectors of economy; it provides essential inputs to agriculture sector and
manufacturing sector.[324] Pakistan's I.T sector is regarded as among
the fastest growing sector's in Pakistan. The [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/World_Economic_Forum"] World Economic Forum, assessing the development of Information and
Communication Technology in the country ranked Pakistan 110th among 139 countries on the 'Networked Readiness
Index 2016'.[325]
As of May 2020, Pakistan has about 82 million internet users, making it the [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_countries_by_number_of_Internet_users"] 9th-largest population of Internet users in the
world.[326][327] The current growth rate and employment trend indicate that Pakistan's Information Communication
Technology (ICT) industry will exceed the $10-billion mark by 2020.[328] The sector employees 12,000 and count's
among top five freelancing nations.[329] The country has also improved its export performance in telecom, computer and
information services, as the share of their exports surged from 8.2pc in 2005–06 to 12.6pc in 2012–13. This growth is
much better than that of China, whose share in services exports was 3pc and 7.7pc for the same period respectively.[330]
Tourism
With its diverse cultures, people, and landscapes, Pakistan attracted around
6.6 million foreign tourists in 2018,[331] which represented a significant decline
since the 1970s when the country received unprecedented numbers of foreign
tourists due to the popular [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hippie_trail"] Hippie trail. The trail attracted thousands of Europeans
and Americans in the 1960s and 1970s who travelled via land through Turkey and
Iran into India through Pakistan.[332] The main destinations of choice for these
tourists were the [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Khyber_Pass"] Khyber Pass, Peshawar, Karachi, Lahore, [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Swat_(Pakistan)"] Swat and
 Rawalpindi.[333] The numbers following the trail declined after the Iranian
Revolution and the [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet%E2%80%93Afghan_War"] Soviet–Afghan War.[334] 
 Lake Saiful Muluk, located at the
northern end of the [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kaghan_Valley"] Kaghan Valley,

from the Buddhist ruins of [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Takht-i-Bahi"] Takht-i-Bahi and Taxila, to the 5,000-year-old cities of
the [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Indus_Valley_Civilization"] Indus Valley Civilization such as [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mohenjo-daro"] Mohenjo-daro and Harappa.[335] Pakistan is
home to several [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mountain_ranges_of_Pakistan"] mountain peaks over 7,000 metres (23,000 feet).[336] The
northern part of Pakistan has many old fortresses, examples of ancient architecture,
and the [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hunza_Valley"] Hunza and Chitral valleys, home to the small pre-Islamic [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kalash_people"] Kalasha
community claiming descent from Alexander the Great.[337] Pakistan's cultural
capital, Lahore, contains many examples of [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mughal_architecture"] Mughal architecture such as the

Fort.  [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Badshahi_Mosque"] Badshahi Mosque was

Infrastructure
Pakistan was recognised as the best country for infrastructure development in South Asia during the [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Monetary_Fund"] IWF and World Bank
annual meetings in 2016.[341]
Page 20
Nuclear power and energy
As of May 2021, [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nuclear_power_in_Pakistan"] nuclear power is provided by six licensed commercial
 nuclear power plants.[342] The [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pakistan_Atomic_Energy_Commission"] Pakistan Atomic Energy Commission (PAEC)
 is solely responsible for operating these power plants, while the Pakistan
Nuclear Regulatory Authority regulates safe usage of the nuclear energy.[343]
The [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Electricity_generation"] electricity generated by commercial nuclear power plants constitutes
 roughly 5.8% of Pakistan's electrical energy, compared to 64.2% from fossil
fuels ([URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Crude_oil"] crude oil and natural gas), 29.9% from [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hydroelectric_power"] hydroelectric power, and 0.1%
from coal.[344][345] Pakistan is one of the four [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_states_with_nuclear_weapons"] nuclear armed states (along
 with India, Israel, and North Korea) that is not a party to the Nuclear Non-
 Proliferation Treaty, but it is a member in good standing of the International
Atomic Energy Agency.[346] 
 Tarbela Dam, the largest earth filled dam
in the world, was constructed in 1968.
 The KANUPP-I, a Candu-type nuclear reactor, was supplied by Canada in 1971—the country's first commercial nuclear
power plant. The Sino-Pakistani nuclear cooperation began in the early 1980s. After a Sino-Pakistani nuclear cooperation
agreement in 1986,[347] China provided Pakistan with a nuclear reactor dubbed [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chashma_Nuclear_Power_Plant"] CHASNUPP-I for energy and the
industrial growth of the country. In 2005 both countries proposed working on a joint energy security plan, calling for a
huge increase in generation capacity to more than 160,000 MWe by 2030. Under its Nuclear Energy Vision 2050, the
Pakistani government plans to increase nuclear power generation capacity to 40,000 MWe,[348] 8,900 MWe of it by
2030.[349]
In June 2008 the nuclear [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chashma_Nuclear_Power_Plant"] commercial complex was expanded with the ground
work of installing and operationalising the [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chashma_Nuclear_Power_Plant"] Chashma-III and [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chashma_Nuclear_Power_Plant"] Chashma–IV reactors
at Chashma, [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Punjab_(Pakistani_province)"] Punjab Province, each with 325–340 MWe and costing [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pakistani_rupee"] ₨
129 billion; from which the [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pakistani_rupee"] ₨ 80 billion came from international sources,
principally China. A further agreement for China's help with the project was
 signed in October 2008, and given prominence as a counter to the US–India
agreement that shortly preceded it. The cost quoted then was US$1.7 billion, with
a foreign loan component of US$1.07 billion. In 2013 Pakistan established a
second commercial [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karachi_Nuclear_Power_Complex"] nuclear complex in [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karachi"] Karachi with plans of additional reactors,
similar to the one in Chashma.[351] The [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Electricity_in_Pakistan"] electrical energy is generated by various
 energy corporations and evenly distributed by the National Electric Power
Regulatory Authority (NEPRA) among the [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Four_Provinces_(Pakistan)"] four provinces. However, the Karachi-
based [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/K-Electric"] K-Electric and the [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Water_and_Power_Development_Authority"] Water and Power Development Authority (WAPDA)
generates much of the electrical energy used in Pakistan in addition to gathering
revenue nationwide.[352] In 2014, Pakistan had an installed [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Electricity_generation"] electricity generation
capacity of ~22,797[URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Megawatt"] MWt.[344] 
Pakistan produced 1,135 megawatts
of [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Renewable_energy"] renewable energy for the month of
October 2016. Pakistan expects to
produce 3,000 megawatts of
renewable energy by the beginning of
2019.[350]
Transport
The transport industry accounts for ~10.5% of the nation's GDP.[353]
Motorways
 Motorways of Pakistan are a network of multiple-lane, high-speed, controlled-
access highways in Pakistan, which are owned, maintained, and operated federally
by Pakistan's [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/National_Highway_Authority"] National Highway Authority. As of 20 February 2020, 1882 km of
motorways are operational, while an additional 1854 km are under construction or
planned. All motorways in Pakistan are pre-fixed with the letter 'M' (for
"Motorway") followed by the unique numerical designation of the specific
highway (with a hyphen in the middle), e.g. "M-1".[354]
Pakistan's motorways are an important part of Pakistan's "National Trade Corridor
Project",[355] which aims to link Pakistan's three [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabian_Sea"] Arabian Sea ports ([URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karachi_Port"] Karachi Port,
 Port Bin Qasim and [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gwadar_Port"] Gwadar Port) to the rest of the country through its national 
The motorway passes through the
 Salt Range mountains
Page 21
highways and motorways network and further north with Afghanistan, [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Central_Asia"] Central Asia and China. The project was planned
in 1990. The [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/China_Pakistan_Economic_Corridor"] China Pakistan Economic Corridor project aims to link [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gwadar_Port"] Gwadar Port and [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kashgar"] Kashgar (China) using Pakistani
motorways, [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/National_Highways_of_Pakistan"] national highways, and expressways.
Highways
Highways form the backbone of Pakistan's transport system; a total road length of 263,942 kilometres (164,006 miles)
accounts for 92% of passengers and 96% of inland freight traffic. Road transport services are largely in the hands of the
 private sector. The National Highway Authority is responsible for the maintenance of national highways and motorways.
The highway and motorway system depends mainly on north–south links connecting the southern ports to the populous
provinces of Punjab and [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Khyber_Pakhtunkhwa"] Khyber-Pakhtunkhwa. Although this network only accounts for 4.6% of total road length,[313] it
carries 85% of the country's traffic.[356][357]
Railways
The [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pakistan_Railways"] Pakistan Railways, under the [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ministry_of_Railways_(Pakistan)"] Ministry of Railways (MoR),  operates the
railroad system. From 1947 until the 1970s the [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pakistan_Railways"] train system was the primary
means of transport until the nationwide constructions of the national highways and
the [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Economic_boom"] economic boom of the [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cars_in_Pakistan"] automotive industry. Beginning in the 1990s there was
a marked shift in traffic from rail to highways; dependence grew on roads after the
introduction of [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Automotive_industry_in_Pakistan"] vehicles in the country. Now the railway's share of inland traffic is
below 8% for passengers and 4% for freight traffic.[313] As personal transportation
began to be dominated by the automobile, total rail track decreased from 8,775 
 Karachi Cantonment railway station
kilometres (5,453 miles) in 1990–91 to 7,791 kilometres (4,841 miles) in
2011.[356][358] Pakistan expects to use the rail service to boost [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Foreign_trade_of_Pakistan"] foreign trade with China, Iran, and Turkey.[359]
Airports
There are an estimated 151 airports and airfields in Pakistan as of 2013—including both the military and the mostly
publicly owned [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Civil_Aviation_Authority_(Pakistan)"] civilian airports.[3] Although [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jinnah_International_Airport"] Jinnah International Airport is the principal international gateway to
Pakistan, the international airports in Lahore, Islamabad, Peshawar, Quetta, Faisalabad, Sialkot, and [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Multan_International_Airport"] Multan also handle
significant amounts of traffic.
 The civil aviation industry is mixed with public and private sectors, which was deregulated in 1993. While the state-
owned [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pakistan_International_Airlines"] Pakistan International Airlines (PIA) is the major and dominant air carrier that carries about 73% of domestic
passengers and all domestic freight, the private airlines such as [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Airblue"] airBlue and [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Air_Indus"] Air Indus, also provide similar services at a
 low cost.
Seaports
Major seaports are in Karachi, Sindh (the [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Port_of_Karachi"] Karachi port, [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Port_Qasim"] Port Qasim).[356][358]
Since the 1990s some seaport operations have been moved to [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Balochistan,_Pakistan"] Balochistan with the
 construction of Gwadar Port, Port of Pasni and Gadani Port.[356][358] Gwadar
Port is the [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Deep_water_port"] deepest sea port of the world.[360] According to the [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/World_Economic_Forum"] WEF's Global
Competitiveness Report, quality ratings of Pakistan's port infrastructure increased
from 3.7 to 4.1 between 2007 and 2016.[361]

The [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Orange_Line_(Lahore_Metro)"] Orange Line Metro Train is an automated [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rapid_transit"] rapid transit system in
Lahore.[362] The Orange line is the first of the three proposed rail lines part for the [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lahore_Metro"] Lahore Metro. The line
spans 27.1 km (16.8 mi) with 25.4 km (15.8 mi) elevated and 1.72 km (1.1 mi) underground and has a cost
of 251.06 billion Rupees ($1.6 billion).[363] The line consists of 26 subway stations and is designed to

carry over 250,000 passengers daily. The line became operational on
25 October 2020.[364]
Metro Bus and BRTs
 Lahore Metrobus is a [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bus_rapid_transit"] bus rapid transit service operating in the city of
Lahore.[365] The Metrobus network's first phase was opened in
February 2013. It was the first Metro bus system in Pakistan. [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Orange_Line_(Lahore_Metro)"] Orange Line Metro Train, [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lahore"] Lahore
 Rawalpindi-Islamabad Metrobus is a 48.1 km (29.9 mi) bus rapid
 transit system operating in the Islamabad-Rawalpindi metropolitan
area. The Metrobus network's first phase was opened on 4 June 2015,
and stretches 22.5 kilometres between [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pak_Secretariat"] Pak Secretariat, in Islamabad,
and [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Saddar,_Rawalpindi"] Saddar in Rawalpindi. The second stage stretches 25.6
 kilometres between the Peshawar Morr Interchange and New
Islamabad International Airport and was inaugurated on 18th April
 2022.[366][367] The system uses e-ticketing and an Intelligent
 Transportation System and is managed by the Punjab Mass Transit
Authority.
 Multan Metrobus is a [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bus_rapid_transit"] bus rapid transit (BRT) system in Multan.[368]
Construction on the line began in May 2015, while operations
commenced on 24 January 2017.[369] 
 Track of Islamabad-Rawalpindi
Metrobus with adjoining station
 Peshawar Bus Rapid Transit (Peshawar BRT) is a [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bus_rapid_transit"] bus rapid transit system in Peshawar, capital of
 Khyber Pakhtunkhwa province. The construction of the project was started in October 2017 and was
inaugurated on 13 August 2020, it is the fourth BRT system in Pakistan.
 Green Line Metrobus is the first phase of [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karachi_Metrobus"] Karachi Metrobus that has been operational since 25 December
2021.[370] The [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Government_of_Pakistan"] Government of Pakistan financed the majority of the project.[371] Construction of the Green
Line began on 26 February 2016.[372]
Faisalabad shuttle train service and [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Faisalabad_Metrobus"] Faisalabad Metrobus are the proposed rapid transit projects in the
 city of Faisalabad. These projects are the part of a mega-project of China–Pakistan Economic
Corridor.[373]
Other Systems
 Karachi Circular Railway is a partially active regional [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Public_transport"] public transit system in Karachi, which serves the
 Karachi metropolitan area. KCR was fully operational between 1969 and 1999. Since 2001, restoration of
the railway and restarting the system had been sought.[374] In November 2020, the KCR partially revived
operations.[375]
A [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mohamedali_Tramways_Company"] tramway service was started in 1884 in [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karachi"] Karachi but was closed in 1975 due to various factors.[376] The
 Sindh Government is planning to restart the tramway services in the city, collaborating with Austrian
experts.[377]
In October 2019, a project for the construction of tramway service in [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lahore"] Lahore has also been signed by the
 Punjab Government. This project will be launched under public-private partnership in a joint venture of
European and Chinese companies along with the Punjab transport department.[378]
Flyovers and underpasses
Many flyovers and underpasses are located in major urban areas of the country to
segregate the flow of traffic. The highest number of flyovers and under passes are
located in Karachi, followed by Lahore.[379] Other cities having flyovers and
underpasses for the regulation of flow of traffic includes [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Islamabad-Rawalpindi_metropolitan_area"] Islamabad-Rawalpindi,
Faisalabad, Gujranwala, Multan, Peshawar, Hyderabad, Quetta, Sargodha,
Bahawalpur, Sukkur, Larkana, [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rahim_Yar_Khan"] Rahim Yar Khan and [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sahiwal"] Sahiwal etc.[380]
Beijing Underpass, [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lahore"] Lahore is the longest underpass of Pakistan with a length of
about 1.3 km (0.81 mi).[381] [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Muslim_Town_Flyover"] Muslim Town Flyover, [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lahore"] Lahore is the longest flyover
of the country with a length of about 2.6 km (1.6 mi).[382] 
Nagan Chowrangi Flyover, [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karachi"] Karachi

Science and technology
Developments in [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Science_and_technology_in_Pakistan"] science and technology have played an important role in Pakistan's
infrastructure and helped the country connect to the rest of the world.[383] Every year,
scientists from around the world are invited by the [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pakistan_Academy_of_Sciences"] Pakistan Academy of Sciences and
 the Pakistan Government to participate in the International Nathiagali Summer College
on Physics.[384] Pakistan hosted an international seminar on "Physics in Developing
Countries" for the International Year of Physics 2005.[385] The Pakistani theoretical
 physicist Abdus Salam won a Nobel Prize in Physics for his work on the electroweak
interaction.[386] Influential publications and critical scientific work in the advancement

Khan, regarded as the founder of the HEU-based [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Zippe-type_centrifuge"] gas-centrifuge [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Uranium_enrichment"] uranium enrichment

 In 2010 Pakistan was ranked 43rd in the world in terms of published scientific papers.[393] The Pakistan Academy of
Sciences, a strong scientific community, plays an influential and vital role in formulating recommendations regarding
science policies for the government.[394] Pakistan was ranked 99th in the [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Global_Innovation_Index"] Global Innovation Index in 2021, up from 107th
in 2020.[395][396][397][398][399]
The 1960s saw the emergence of an active [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Asian_Space_Race"] space program led by [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_and_Upper_Atmosphere_Research_Commission"] SUPARCO that produced advances in domestic
rocketry, electronics, and aeronomy. The [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_and_Upper_Atmosphere_Research_Commission"] space program recorded a few notable feats and achievements. The successful
launch of its [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rehbar-I"] first rocket into space made Pakistan the first South Asian country to have achieved such a task.[400]
Successfully producing and launching the nation's [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Badr-I"] first space satellite in 1990, Pakistan became the first Muslim country
and second South Asian country to put a satellite into space.[401]

As an aftermath of the [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Indo-Pakistani_War_of_1971"] 1971 war with India, the clandestine
 crash program developed [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nuclear_weapon"] atomic weapons partly motivated by
fear and to prevent any [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Foreign_interventionism"] foreign intervention, while ushering in
the [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Atomic_Age"] atomic age in the [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Post_Cold_War_era"] post cold war era.[184] Competition with
India and tensions eventually led to Pakistan's decision to
 conduct underground [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chagai-I"] nuclear tests in 1998, thus becoming the
 seventh country in the world to successfully develop nuclear
weapons.[403]
Pakistan is the first and only Muslim country that maintains an
 active research presence in Antarctica.[404] Since 1991
Pakistan has maintained two summer research stations and one
weather observatory on the continent and plans to open another

82 million Internet users and is ranked as one of the top countries that have registered a high growth rate in Internet
penetration as of 2020.[326] Key publications have been produced by Pakistan, and domestic software development has

As of May 2020, Pakistan has about 82 million internet users, making it the [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_countries_by_number_of_Internet_users"] 9th-largest population of Internet users in the

on [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Information_technology_in_Pakistan"] information technology projects, with emphasis on [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/E-Government_in_Pakistan"] e-government, human resources, and infrastructure
development.[407]
Education
The [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Constitution_of_Pakistan"] constitution of Pakistan requires the state to provide [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Free_education"] free primary and secondary education.[408]
At the time of the [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Creation_of_Pakistan"] establishment of Pakistan as a state, the country had only one
university, [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/University_of_the_Punjab"] Punjab University in Lahore.[409] Very soon the [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Government_of_Pakistan"] Pakistan government
 established public universities in each of the four provinces, including Sindh
University (1949), [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/University_of_Peshawar"] Peshawar University (1950), [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/University_of_Karachi"] Karachi University (1953), and
 Balochistan University (1970). Pakistan has a large network of both [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Public_universities"] public and
 private universities, which includes collaboration between the [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Universities_in_Pakistan"] universities aimed at
providing research and [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Higher_education_in_Pakistan"] higher education opportunities in the country, although
there is concern about the low quality of teaching in many of the newer
 schools.[410] It is estimated that there are 3,193 technical and vocational
institutions in Pakistan,[411] and there are also [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Madrassas_in_Pakistan"] madrassahs that provide free [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/University_of_Sargodha"] Central Library of University of
Islamic education and offer free board and lodging to students, who come mainly Sargodha
from the poorer strata of society.[412] Strong public pressure and popular criticism
over [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pakistan_Taleban"] extremists' usage of madrassahs for recruitment, the Pakistan government has
made repeated efforts to [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Education_reform"] regulate and monitor the quality of education in the madrassahs.[413]
 Education in Pakistan is divided into six main levels: nursery
(preparatory classes);  primary (grades one through five);  [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Middle_school"] middle
(grades six through eight); [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/High_school_(upper_secondary)"] matriculation (grades nine and ten, leading
to the [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Secondary_School_Certificate"] secondary certificate); [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Community_college"] intermediate (grades eleven and twelve,
leading to a [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Higher_Secondary_(School)_Certificate"] higher secondary certificate); and university programmes
leading to graduate and postgraduate degrees.[411] There is a network
of [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Schools_in_Pakistan"] private schools that constitutes a parallel secondary education
 system based on a curriculum set and administered by the Cambridge
International Examinations of the United Kingdom. Some students
choose to take the [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ordinary_Level"] O-level and [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/GCE_Advanced_Level"] A level exams conducted by the
 British Council.[414] According to the International Schools
Consultancy, Pakistan has 439 international schools.[415]  Literacy rate in Pakistan 1951–2018
As a result of initiatives taken in 2007, the [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/English_medium_education"] English medium education
 has been made compulsory in all schools across the country.[416] In 2012, Malala
Yousafzai, a campaigner for [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Female_education"] female education, was shot by a [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tehrik-i_Taliban_Pakistan"] Taliban gunman in
retaliation for her activism.[417] Yousafzai went on to become the youngest ever
Nobel laureate for her global education-related advocacy.[418] Additional reforms
enacted in 2013 required all educational institutions in Sindh to begin offering
Chinese language courses, reflecting China's growing role as a superpower and its
 increasing influence in Pakistan.[419] The literacy rate of the population is 62.3%
as of 2018. The rate of male literacy is 72.5% while the rate of female literacy is
51.8%.[420] Literacy rates vary by region and particularly by sex; as one example,
in tribal areas female literacy is 9.5%,[421] while [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Azad_Jammu_%26_Kashmir"] Azad Jammu & Kashmir has a
literacy rate of 74%.[422] With the advent of computer literacy in 1995, the 
Malala Yousafzai at the Women of
the World festival in 2014.
government launched a nationwide initiative in 1998 with the aim of eradicating [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Illiteracy"] illiteracy and providing a basic education
to all children.[423] Through various educational reforms, by 2015 the [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Minister_for_Education_(Pakistan)"] Ministry of Education expected to attain 100%
enrollment levels among children of primary school age and a literacy rate of ~86% among people aged over 10.[424]
[URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Institute_of_Social_and_Policy_Sciences"] Pakistan is currently spending 2.3 percent of its GDP on education;[425] which according to the Institute of Social and
Policy Sciences is one of the lowest in South Asia.[426]
Demographics
Page 25
Pakistan had a population of 213,222,917 according to the final
results of the [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/2017_Census_of_Pakistan"] 2017 Census of Pakistan.[427][428][429] This figure
 includes Pakistan's four provinces, Islamabad Capital Territory, Azad
 Kashmir, and Gilgit-Baltistan. Pakistan is the world's fifth-most
populated country.[430]
Between 1951 and 2017, Pakistan's population expanded over
sixfold, going from 33.7 million to 207.7 million. The country has a
relatively high, although declining, growth rate supported by high
birth rates and low death rates. Between 1998 and 2017, the average
annual population growth rate stood at +2.40%.
Dramatic social changes have led to urbanisation and the emergence Historical population of Pakistan
of two megacities: [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karachi"] Karachi and Lahore. The country's urban
population more than tripled between 1981 and 2017 (from 23.8
million to 75.7 million, as Pakistan's [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Urbanization"] urbanization rate rose from 28.2% to 36.4%. Even with this, the nation's urbanization
rate remains one of [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Urbanization_by_country"] the lowest in the world, and in 2017, over 130 million Pakistanis (making up nearly 65% of the
population) lived in [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rural_area"] rural areas.
Due to a high [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Total_fertility_rate"] fertility rate, estimated at 3.5 in 2022, Pakistan has one of the world's [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_countries_by_median_age"] youngest populations. The 2017
census recorded that 40.3% of the country's population was under the age of 15, while only 3.7% of Pakistanis were aged
65 or more.[431] The [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Median_age"] median age of the country was 19,[431] while its [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sex_ratio"] sex ratio was recorded to be 105 males per 100
females.[427]
The [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Demographic_history_of_Pakistan"] demographic history of Pakistan from the ancient [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Indus_Valley_Civilization"] Indus Valley Civilization to the modern era includes the arrival and
settlement of many cultures and ethnic groups in the modern region of Pakistan from [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Eurasia"] Eurasia and the nearby [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Middle_East"] Middle East.
 Because of this, Pakistan has a multicultural, multilinguistic, and multiethnic society. Despite Urdu being Pakistan's lingua
franca, estimates on how many languages are spoken in the country range from 75 to 85,[432][433] and in 2017, the
country's three largest [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ethnolinguistic_group"] ethnolinguistic groups were the [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Punjabis"] Punjabis (making up 38.8% of the total population), the [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pashtuns"] Pashtuns
(18.2%), and the [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sindhis"] Sindhis (14.6%).[434] Pakistan is also thought to have the world's fourth-largest [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_countries_by_refugee_population"] refugee population,
estimated at 1.4 million in mid-2021 by the UNHCR.[435]
Languages
More than sixty languages are spoken in Pakistan, including a number of
 provincial languages. Urdu—the lingua franca and a symbol of Muslim
identity and national unity—is the national language and understood by over
75% of Pakistanis. It is the main medium of communication in the country,
but the primary language of only 7% of the population.[436][437] Urdu and
English are the [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Official_language"] official languages of Pakistan. English is primarily used in  Population Density per square kilometre of
official business and government, and in legal contracts;[150] the local variety each Pakistani District as of the 2017
is known as [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pakistani_English"] Pakistani English. Punjabi, the most common language and the
 first language of 38.78% of the population,[436] is mostly spoken in the 
Pakistan Census
Punjab. [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Saraiki_language"] Saraiki is mainly spoken in South Punjab, and [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hindko"] Hindko is
predominant in the [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hazara_Division"] Hazara region of [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Khyber_Pakhtunkhwa"] Khyber Pakhtunkhwa. [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pashto"] Pashto is the
provincial language of Khyber Pakhtunkhwa. [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sindhi_language"] Sindhi is commonly spoken in
Sindh, while [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Balochi_language"] Balochi is dominant in Balochistan. Brahui, a Dravidian
language, is spoken by the [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Brahui_people"] Brahui people who live in Balochistan.[438][439]
There are also speakers of [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gujarati_language"] Gujarati in Karachi.[440] Marwari, a Rajasthani
language, is also spoken in parts of Sindh. Various languages such as Shina,
Balti, and [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Burushaski"] Burushaski are spoken in [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gilgit-Baltistan"] Gilgit-Baltistan, whilst languages such as
Pahari, Gojri, and [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kashmiri_language"] Kashmiri are spoken by many in [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Azad_Kashmir"] Azad Kashmir. 
Population of each Pakistani District as of
 Arabic is officially recognised by the [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Constitution_of_Pakistan"] constitution of Pakistan. It declares in  the 2017 Pakistan Census
article 31 No. 2 that "The State shall endeavour, as respects the Muslims of
Pakistan (a) to make the teaching of the Holy Quran and Islamiat
compulsory, to encourage and facilitate the learning of Arabic
language ..."[441]

Even after partition in 1947, Indian Muslims continued to migrate to

and half a million other undocumented people live in Pakistan. They are claimed
to be from other areas such as Myanmar, Iran, Iraq, and Africa.[446]

complaining that many of the refugee women had been raped by Indian soldiers and that they were forced out of their
homes by the soldiers.[451]
Ethnic groups
The major ethnic groups are [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Punjabi_people"] Punjabis (44.7% of the country's
population),  Pashtuns, also known as Pathans (15.4%),  [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sindhi_people"] Sindhis
(14.1%),  [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Saraiki_people"] Saraikis (8.4%),  [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Muhajir_(Pakistan)"] Muhajirs (the [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Indian_Muslims_in_Pakistan"] Indian emigrants, mostly
Urdu-speaking), who make up 7.6% of the population, and the [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Baloch_people"] Baloch
with 3.6%.[3] The remaining 6.3% consist of a number of ethnic
minorities such as the Brahuis,[438] the Hindkowans, the various
peoples of Gilgit-Baltistan, the Kashmiris, the [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Siddi"] Sheedis (who are of
African descent),[452] and the Hazaras.[453] There is also a large
 Pakistani diaspora worldwide, numbering over seven million,[454]
which has been recorded as the sixth largest diaspora in the world.[455]

Urbanisation
Since [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Independence_of_Pakistan"] achieving independence as a result of the [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Partition_of_India"] partition of India, the [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Urbanisation_in_Pakistan"] urbanisation
has increased exponentially, with several different causes. The majority of the
population in the south resides along the Indus River, with [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karachi"] Karachi the most
populous commercial city.[456] In the east, west, and north, most of the population
lives in an arc formed by the cities of Lahore, Faisalabad, Rawalpindi, Islamabad,
Sargodha, Gujranwala, Sialkot, Gujrat, Jhelum, Sheikhupura, Nowshera, Mardan,
and Peshawar. During the period 1990–2008, city dwellers made up 36% of
Pakistan's population, making it the most urbanised nation in South Asia.
Furthermore, more than 50% of Pakistanis live in towns of 5,000 people or
more.[457] Immigration, from both within and outside the country, is regarded as 
 Kalma Underpass, [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lahore"] Lahore
one of the main factors contributing to urbanisation in Pakistan. One analysis of the [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1998_Pakistan_Census"] 1998 national census highlighted the
Page 27
significance of the [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Partition_of_India"] partition of India in the 1940s as it relates to urban change in Pakistan.[458]During and after the
independence period, [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Muhajir_(Urdu-speaking_people)"] Urdu speaking Muslims from India migrated in large numbers to Pakistan, especially to the port city
of Karachi, which is today the largest metropolis in Pakistan. [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Immigration_to_Pakistan"] Migration from other countries, mainly from those nearby,
has further accelerated the process of urbanisation in Pakistani cities. Inevitably, the rapid urbanisation caused by these
large population movements has also created new political and socio-economic challenges. In addition to immigration,
economic trends such as the green revolution and political developments, among a host of other factors, are also important
causes of urbanisation.[458]

Religion
The state religion in Pakistan is Islam.[464] Freedom of
religion is guaranteed by the [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Constitution_of_Pakistan"] Constitution of Pakistan,
which provides all its citizens the right to profess, practice
and propagate their religion subject to law, public order,
and morality.[465] 
[URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Religion_in_Pakistan"] Religions in Pakistan (2017 Census)[436][460][461][462][463]
Religions  Percent
 Islam     96.47%
 Hinduism    2.14%
 Christianity    1.27%
 others/non-religious    0.11%
The majority of Pakistanis are Muslims (96.47%)
followed by [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hinduism_in_Pakistan"] Hindus (2.14%) and [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Christianity_in_Pakistan"] Christians (1.27%). There are also people in Pakistan who follow other religions, such as
Sikhism, Buddhism, [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jainism_in_Pakistan"] Jainism and the minority of [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Parsi"] Parsi (who follow Zoroastrianism). The [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kalash_people"] Kalash people maintain a unique
identity and religion within Pakistan.[466]
Hinduism is mostly associated with Sindhis, and Pakistan hosts major events such as the [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hinglaj_Mata_mandir#Annual_pilgrimage_(Hinglaj_Yatra_or_Theerth_Yatra)"] Hinglaj Yatra pilgrimage. Hindu
temples may be found [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hinduism_in_Sindh_Province"] throughout Sindh, where the dharma features prominently. Many Hindus in Pakistan complain
about the prospect of religious violence against them and being treated like second-class citizens, and many have
emigrated to India or further abroad.[467]
In addition, some Pakistanis also do not profess any faith (such as [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Atheism"] atheists and agnostics) in Pakistan. According to the
1998 census, people who did not state their religion accounted for 0.5% of the population.
Islam
Islam is the dominant religion.[468] About 96.47% of [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pakistanis"] Pakistanis are Muslim, according to the 2017 Census.[436] Pakistan
has the second-largest number of [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Muslim"] Muslims in the world after Indonesia.[469] and home for (10.5%) of the world's Muslim
population.[470] The majority of them are [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sunni_Islam"] Sunni and mostly follow [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sufism"] Sufism (estimated between 75 and 95%)[471][472]
while [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shia_Islam"] Shias represent between 5–25%.[471][3][473] In 2019, the Shia population in Pakistan was estimated to be 42 million
out of total population of 210 million.[474] Pakistan also has the largest Muslim city in the world (Karachi).[475]
Page 28
Ahmadiyya Proportion of each Pakistani
District in 2017 according to the Pakistan
Bureau of Statistics
 Faisal Mosque, built in 1986
 by Turkish architect Vedat
Dalokay on behalf of [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/House_of_Saud"] King
 Faisal bin Abdul-Aziz of Saudi
Arabia 
The Ahmadis, a small minority representing 0.22–2% of Pakistan's population,[476] are
officially considered non-Muslims by virtue of the [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Second_Amendment_to_the_Constitution_of_Pakistan"] constitutional amendment.[477] The
 Ahmadis are particularly persecuted, especially since 1974 when they were [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Second_Amendment_to_the_Constitution_of_Pakistan"] banned from
calling themselves Muslims. In 1984, Ahmadiyya places of worship were banned from
 being called "mosques".[478] As of 2012, 12% of Pakistani Muslims self-identify as non-
denominational Muslims.[479] There are also several [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quraniyoon"] Quraniyoon communities.[480]
They are mainly concentratd in the [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lalian_Tehsil"] Lalian Tehsil, [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chiniot_District"] Chiniot District, where approximately
13% of the population.[481]
Sufism, a mystical Islamic tradition, has a long history and a large following among the Sunni Muslims in Pakistan, at both
the academic and popular levels. Popular Sufi culture is centered around gatherings and celebrations at the shrines of saints
 and annual festivals that feature Sufi music and dance. Two Sufis whose shrines receive much national attention are Ali
Hajweri in [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lahore"] Lahore (c. 12th century)[482] and [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shahbaz_Qalander"] Shahbaz Qalander in Sehwan, Sindh (c. 12th century).[483]
There are two levels of [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sufism"] Sufism in Pakistan. The first is the 'populist' Sufism of the rural population. This level of Sufism
involves belief in intercession through saints, veneration of their shrines, and forming bonds (Mureed) with a pir (saint).
Many rural Pakistani Muslims associate with [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pir_(Sufism)"] pirs and seek their intercession.[484] The second level of Sufism in Pakistan
is 'intellectual Sufism', which is growing among the urban and educated population. They are influenced by the writings of
Sufis such as the medieval theologian [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Al-Ghazali"] al-Ghazali, the Sufi reformer [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shaykh_Ahmad_Sirhindi"] Shaykh Aḥmad Sirhindi, and [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shah_Wali_Allah"] Shah Wali Allah.[485]
Contemporary Islamic fundamentalists criticise Sufism's popular character, which in their view does not accurately reflect
the teachings and practice of [URL: "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Muhammad"] Muhammad and his companions.[486]
Hinduism 
 Hinduism is the second-largest
religion in Pakistan after Islam and is
followed by 2.14% of the population
according to the 2017 census.[5][488]
According to the 2010 Pew report,
Pakistan had the fifth-largest Hindu
population in the world.[489] In the

in 2017 according to the Pakistan Bureau
